Text: It is known that Maxwell’s electrodynamics—as usually understood at the, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: present time—when applied to moving bodies, leads to asymmetries which do, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: not appear to be inherent in the phenomena. Take, for example, the recipro-, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: cal electrodynamic action of a magnet and a conductor. The observable phe-, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: nomenon here depends only on the relative motion of the conductor and the, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: magnet, whereas the customary view draws a sharp distinction between the two, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: cases in which either the one or the other of these bodies is in motion. For if the, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: magnet is in motion and the conductor at rest, there arises in the neighbour-, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: hood of the magnet an electric ﬁeld with a certain deﬁnite energy, producing, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: a current at the places where parts of the conductor are situated. But if the, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: magnet is stationary and the conductor in motion, no electric ﬁeld arises in the, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: neighbourhood of the magnet. In the conductor, however, we ﬁnd an electro-, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: motive force, to which in itself there is no corresponding energy, but which gives, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: rise—assuming equality of relative motion in the two cases discussed—to elec-, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: tric currents of the same path and intensity as those produced by the electric, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: forces in the former case., Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: Examples of this sort, together with the unsuccessful attempts to discover, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: any motion of the earth relatively to the “light medium,” suggest that the, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: phenomena of electrodynamics as well as of mechanics possess no properties, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: corresponding to the idea of absolute rest. They suggest rather that, as has, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: already been shown to the ﬁrst order of small quantities, the same laws of, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: electrodynamics and optics will be valid for all frames of reference for which the, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: equations of mechanics hold good., Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496

Text: As to the interpretation of these equations we make the following remarks: Let, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: a point charge of electricity have the magnitude “one” when measured in the, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: stationary system K, i.e. let it when at rest in the stationary system exert a, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496

Text: By the principle of relativity this electric charge is also of the magnitude “one”, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: when measured in the moving system. If this quantity of electricity is at rest, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: relatively to the stationary system, then by deﬁnition the vector (X, Y, Z) is, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: equal to the force acting upon it. If the quantity of electricity is at rest relatively, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
Text: to the moving system (at least at the relevant instant), then the force acting, Font: CMR10, Size: 9.962599754333496
